<p>The <b>National Railroad Passenger Corporation</b>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doing_business_as" title="Doing business as">doing business as</a> <b>Amtrak</b> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/æ/ short 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/æ/ short 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span></span>/</a></span></span>, is a passenger <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport#Passenger_trains" title="Rail transport">railroad</a> service that provides medium- and long-distance <a href="/wiki/Inter-city_rail" title="Inter-city rail">intercity</a> service in the <a href="/wiki/Contiguous_United_States" title="Contiguous United States">contiguous United States</a>. Founded in 1971 to take over most of the remaining U.S. passenger rail services, it is partially government funded yet operated and managed as a for-profit corporation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Amtrak serves more than 500 destinations in 46 states and three <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canadian provinces</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amtrak.com_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-amtrak.com-2">[2]</a></sup> operating more than 300 trains each day over 21,300 miles (34,000 km) of track. Some track sections allow trains to run as fast as 150 mph (240 km/h). In <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_year" title="Fiscal year">fiscal year</a> 2015, Amtrak served 30.8 million passengers and had $2.185 billion in revenue,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fact2015_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fact2015-3">[3]</a></sup> while employing more than 20,000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Nearly two-thirds of passengers come from the 10 largest metropolitan areas; 83% of passengers travel on routes shorter than 400 miles (644 km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brookings2013_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brookings2013-5">[5]</a></sup> Its headquarters is at <a href="/wiki/Union_Station_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Union Station (Washington, D.C.)">Union Station</a> in Washington, D.C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>The name "Amtrak" is a <a href="/wiki/Portmanteau" title="Portmanteau">portmanteau</a> of the words "America" and "trak", the latter itself a <a href="/wiki/Sensational_spelling" title="Sensational spelling">sensational spelling</a> of "track".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-web-amtrak1_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-web-amtrak1-8">[8]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_rail_transport_in_the_United_States" title="History of rail transport in the United States">History of rail transport in the United States</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Amtrak">Pre-Amtrak</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pre-Amtrak">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Privately_operated_passenger_rail_service">Privately operated passenger rail service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Privately operated passenger rail service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Illinois_Central_Railroad_Panama_Limited_diesel_streamliner.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="969" data-file-width="1540" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Illinois_Central_Railroad_Panama_Limited_diesel_streamliner.JPG/220px-Illinois_Central_Railroad_Panama_Limited_diesel_streamliner.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Illinois_Central_Railroad_Panama_Limited_diesel_streamliner.JPG/330px-Illinois_Central_Railroad_Panama_Limited_diesel_streamliner.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Illinois_Central_Railroad_Panama_Limited_diesel_streamliner.JPG/440px-Illinois_Central_Railroad_Panama_Limited_diesel_streamliner.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boston_and_Maine_Railroad_-1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="660" data-file-width="1024" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Boston_and_Maine_Railroad_-1.jpg/220px-Boston_and_Maine_Railroad_-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Boston_and_Maine_Railroad_-1.jpg/330px-Boston_and_Maine_Railroad_-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Boston_and_Maine_Railroad_-1.jpg/440px-Boston_and_Maine_Railroad_-1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Rebel_streamlined_train.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="466" data-file-width="759" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/The_Rebel_streamlined_train.JPG/220px-The_Rebel_streamlined_train.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/The_Rebel_streamlined_train.JPG/330px-The_Rebel_streamlined_train.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/The_Rebel_streamlined_train.JPG/440px-The_Rebel_streamlined_train.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

The <a href="/wiki/Gulf,_Mobile_and_Northern_Railroad" title="Gulf, Mobile and Northern Railroad">Gulf, Mobile and Northern Railroad</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Rebel_(train)" title="Rebel (train)">Rebel</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Streamliner" title="Streamliner">streamliner</a> train</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From the mid-19th century until about 1920, nearly all intercity travelers in the United States moved by rail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schafer_2001_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schafer_2001-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Historically, U.S. passenger trains were owned and operated by the same privately owned companies that operated freight trains. About 65,000 railroad passenger cars operated in 1929.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carper-1968-112-113_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carper-1968-112-113-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>From 1920 into the later 20th century, passenger rail's popularity diminished and there was a series of pullbacks and tentative recoveries. Rail passenger revenues declined dramatically between 1920 and 1934 because of the rise of the automobile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schafer_2001_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schafer_2001-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In the same period, many travelers were lost to interstate bus companies such as <a href="/wiki/Greyhound_Lines" title="Greyhound Lines">Greyhound Lines</a>. However, in the mid-1930s, railroads reignited popular imagination with service improvements and new, diesel-powered <a href="/wiki/Streamliner" title="Streamliner">streamliners</a>, such as the gleaming silver <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_Zephyr" title="Pioneer Zephyr">Pioneer Zephyr</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Flying_Yankee" title="Flying Yankee">Flying Yankee</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schafer_2001_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schafer_2001-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Even with the improvements, on a relative basis, traffic continued to decline, and by 1940 railroads held just 67 percent of passenger-miles in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schafer_2001_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-Schafer_2001-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> broke the malaise; passenger traffic soared sixfold thanks to troop movements and restrictions on automobile fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schafer_2001_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-Schafer_2001-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> After the war, railroads rejuvenated the overworked and neglected fleets with fast and often luxurious streamliners, as epitomized by the <i><a href="/wiki/Super_Chief" title="Super Chief">Super Chief</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/California_Zephyr" title="California Zephyr">California Zephyr</a></i>, which inspired the last major resurgence in passenger rail travel.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The postwar resurgence was short-lived. In 1946, there remained 45 percent fewer passenger trains than in 1929,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schafer_2001_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-Schafer_2001-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and the decline quickened despite railroad optimism. Passengers disappeared and so did trains. Few trains generated profits; most produced losses. Broad-based passenger rail deficits appeared as early as 1948,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schafer_2001_9-6"><a href="#cite_note-Schafer_2001-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and by the mid-1950s, railroads claimed aggregate annual losses on passenger services of more than $700 million (almost $5 billion in 2005 dollars when adjusted for inflation).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carper-1968-112-113_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carper-1968-112-113-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw-williamson1_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-mw-williamson1-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>By 1965, only 10,000 rail passenger cars were in operation, 85 percent fewer than in 1929.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carper-1968-112-113_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Carper-1968-112-113-10">[10]</a></sup> Passenger service was provided on only 75,000 miles (120,000 km) of track, a stark decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carper-1968-112-113_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-Carper-1968-112-113-10">[10]</a></sup> The 1960s also saw the end of <a href="/wiki/Railway_post_office" title="Railway post office">railway post office</a> revenues, which had helped some of the remaining trains break even.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WreckPennCtrl_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-WreckPennCtrl-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes_of_decline">Causes of decline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Causes of decline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The causes of the decline of passenger rail in the United States were complex. Until 1920, rail was the only practical form of intercity transport, but the industry was subject to government regulation and labor inflexibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MorganD_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-MorganD-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SlasonT_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-SlasonT-15">[15]</a></sup> By 1930, the railroad companies had constructed, with private funding, a vast and relatively efficient transportation network.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>When the federal government began to construct the <a href="/wiki/National_Highway_System_(United_States)" title="National Highway System (United States)">National Highway System</a>, the railroads found themselves faced with unprecedented competition for passengers and freight with automobiles, buses, trucks, and aircraft, all of which were heavily subsidized by the government road and airport building programs. In 1916, the amount of track in the United States peaked at 254,251 miles (409,177 km), compared to 140,695 miles (226,427 km) in 2007 (although it remained the largest rail network of any country in the world).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some rail routes had been built primarily to facilitate the sale of stock in the railroad companies; they were redundant from the beginning.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> These were the first to be abandoned as the railroads' financial positions deteriorated, and the rails were routinely removed to save money on taxes. Many <a href="/wiki/Right-of-way_(transportation)" title="Right-of-way (transportation)">rights-of-way</a> were destroyed by being broken up and built over, but others remained the property of the railroad or were taken over by local or state authorities and turned into <a href="/wiki/Rail_trail" title="Rail trail">rail trails</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_regulation">Government regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Government regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>From about 1910 to 1921, the federal government introduced a populist rate-setting scheme. During <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, the railroads proved incapable of functioning as a cohesive network. This forced the United States Government to <a href="/wiki/United_States_Railroad_Administration" title="United States Railroad Administration">nationalize the rail industry temporarily</a>. In the 1920s, railroad profits stagnated, many redundant and unprofitable lines were abandoned, and many passenger facilities were allowed to fall into a cycle of <a href="/wiki/Deferred_maintenance" title="Deferred maintenance">deferred maintenance</a>, all of which, in small ways, drove passengers away, either by higher fares or less appealing service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SlasonT_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-SlasonT-15">[15]</a></sup> At the same time, the rise in popularity of the automobile and U.S. highways, such as the <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Highway" title="Lincoln Highway">Lincoln Highway</a>, began to eat away at local rail passenger traffic. Increases in labor costs also further hindered the railroads' ability to make profits on smaller and more sparsely populated lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SlasonT_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-SlasonT-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>The primary regulatory authority affecting railroads, beginning in the late 19th century, was the <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Commerce_Commission" title="Interstate Commerce Commission">Interstate Commerce Commission</a> (ICC). The ICC played a leading role in rate-setting, which, at times, hindered railroads' ability to be profitable in the passenger market. In the 1930s, train speeds were ever increasing, but no advancements were being made in signaling and safety systems to prevent collisions. This led to the horrific <a href="/wiki/Naperville_train_disaster" title="Naperville train disaster">Naperville train disaster</a> of 1946 and other crashes in New York in 1950. In 1947,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dieselization_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-dieselization-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gruber-solomon_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-gruber-solomon-19">[19]</a></sup> the ICC issued an order requiring U.S. railroads within four years to install <a href="/wiki/Automatic_train_stop" title="Automatic train stop">automatic train stop</a>, <a href="/wiki/Automatic_train_control" title="Automatic train control">automatic train control</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Cab_signalling" title="Cab signalling">cab signaling</a> wherever any trains would travel at 80 mph (130 km/h) or faster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Signals_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Signals-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Such technology was not widely implemented outside the Northeast,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asktrains08_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-asktrains08-21">[21]</a></sup> effectively placing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Speed_limits_in_the_United_States_(rail)#Signal_speeds" title="Speed limits in the United States (rail)">speed limit</a> in other areas, which is still in effect today, and why the 79 mph (127 km/h) maximum passenger-train speed is common in the United States. In 1958, the ICC was granted authority to allow or reject modifications and eliminations of passenger routes, a process known as "train-offs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Many routes required beneficial pruning, but the ICC operated at a glacial pace, delaying action by an average of eight months. By the time it authorized modifications, the ICC insisted that unsuccessful routes be merged with profitable ones. Thus, fast, popular rail service was transformed into slow, unpopular service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MorganD_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-MorganD-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ICC was even more critical of corporate mergers. Many combinations which railroads sought to complete were delayed for years and even decades, such as the merger of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Central_Railroad" title="New York Central Railroad">New York Central Railroad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Railroad" title="Pennsylvania Railroad">Pennsylvania Railroad</a>, into what eventually became <a href="/wiki/Penn_Central_Transportation_Company" title="Penn Central Transportation Company">Penn Central</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Delaware,_Lackawanna_and_Western_Railroad" title="Delaware, Lackawanna and Western Railroad">Delaware, Lackawanna and Western Railroad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erie_Railroad" title="Erie Railroad">Erie Railroad</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Erie_Lackawanna_Railway" title="Erie Lackawanna Railway">Erie Lackawanna Railway</a>. By the time the ICC approved the mergers in the 1960s, slower trains, years of deteriorating equipment and station facilities, and the flight of passengers to air and automobile transportation had taken their toll, and the mergers were unsuccessful at preserving these railroads' passenger train service.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It is important to note the Erie Lackawanna was never a major hauler of passengers, nor its predecessor roads, and was mostly a freight railroad. The Penn Central merger was a failure because it merged two large struggling railroads on paper only; two separate management structures remained with little or no integration of assets or management of the former Pennsylvania Railroad and New York Central system. The massive overhead costs of this operating scheme played a far greater role in the Penn Central failure than any actions taken by the ICC or any other US Government agency.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxation">Taxation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Taxation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>At the same time, railroads carried a substantial tax burden. A World War II–era excise tax of 15% on passenger rail travel survived until 1962.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Local governments, far from providing needed support to passenger rail, viewed rail infrastructure as a ready source for property tax revenues. In one extreme example, in 1959, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Northern_Railway_(U.S.)" title="Great Northern Railway (U.S.)">Great Northern Railway</a>, which owned 0.34% of the land in <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_County,_Montana" title="Lincoln County, Montana">Lincoln County, Montana</a>, was assessed more than 91% of all school taxes in the county.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MorganD_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-MorganD-14">[14]</a></sup> To this day, railroads are generally taxed at a higher rate than other industries, and the rates vary greatly from state to state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Labor-related_issues">Labor-related issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Labor-related issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="mw-stack" style="box-sizing: border-box; float:right;">
<div style="overflow: hidden; margin: 1px;">
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_Logo.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="142" data-file-width="479" height="65" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Amtrak_Logo.svg/220px-Amtrak_Logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Amtrak_Logo.svg/330px-Amtrak_Logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Amtrak_Logo.svg/440px-Amtrak_Logo.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_Logo.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Amtrak's former logo (1971–2000), nicknamed the "inverted arrow" or the "pointless arrow". It is still in use on some signs, stations and rolling stock.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Union_Station_from_Columbus_Circle,_Washington,_D.C._2011.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2527" data-file-width="4272" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Union_Station_from_Columbus_Circle%2C_Washington%2C_D.C._2011.jpg/220px-Union_Station_from_Columbus_Circle%2C_Washington%2C_D.C._2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Union_Station_from_Columbus_Circle%2C_Washington%2C_D.C._2011.jpg/330px-Union_Station_from_Columbus_Circle%2C_Washington%2C_D.C._2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Union_Station_from_Columbus_Circle%2C_Washington%2C_D.C._2011.jpg/440px-Union_Station_from_Columbus_Circle%2C_Washington%2C_D.C._2011.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Union_Station_from_Columbus_Circle,_Washington,_D.C._2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Union_Station_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Union Station (Washington, D.C.)">Union Station</a>, the headquarters of Amtrak in Washington, D.C.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Railroads also faced antiquated work rules and inflexible relationships with trade unions. Work rules did not adapt to <a href="/wiki/Technological_change" title="Technological change">technological change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MorganD_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-MorganD-14">[14]</a></sup> Average train speeds had doubled from 1919 to 1959, but unions resisted efforts to modify their existing 100-to-150-mile (160 to 240 km) work days. As a result, railroad workers' average work days were roughly cut in half, from 5–7½ hours in 1919 to 2½–3¾ hours in 1959.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Between 1947 and 1957, passenger railroad financial efficiency dropped by 42% per mile.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsidized_competition">Subsidized competition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Subsidized competition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>While passenger rail faced internal and governmental pressures, new challenges appeared that undermined the dominance of passenger rail: highways and commercial aviation. The passenger rail industry declined as governments put money into the construction of highways and government-owned airports and the air traffic control system.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>As automobiles became more attainable to most Americans, the freedom, increased convenience and individualization of automobile travel became the norm. Government actively began to respond with funds from its treasury and later with fuel-tax funds to build a non-profit network of roads not subject to property taxation. Highways then surpassed the for-profit rail network that the railroads had built in previous generations with corporate capital and government land grants. All told between 1921 and 1955 governmental entities, using taxpayer money and in response to taxpayer demand, financed more than $93 billion worth of pavement, construction, and maintenance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MorganD_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-MorganD-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1950s, affordable commercial aviation expanded as the <a href="/wiki/Jet_Age" title="Jet Age">Jet Age</a> arrived. Governmental entities built urban and suburban airports, funded construction of highways to provide access to the airports, and provided air traffic control services.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Loss_of_U.S._Mail_contracts">Loss of U.S. Mail contracts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Loss of U.S. Mail contracts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Until 1966, most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Postal_Service" title="U.S. Postal Service">U.S. Postal Service</a> mail was transported on passenger trains. The mail contracts kept many passenger trains economically viable. In 1966, the U.S. Postal Service switched to trucks and airplanes, subsidizing planes instead of trains, which no longer had mail as a source of revenue.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_railroads_eligible_to_participate_in_the_formation_of_Amtrak" title="List of railroads eligible to participate in the formation of Amtrak">List of railroads eligible to participate in the formation of Amtrak</a></div>

</div>
</div>
<p>In the late 1960s, the end of passenger rail in the United States seemed near. First had come the requests for termination of services; then came the bankruptcy filings. The <a href="/wiki/Pullman_Company" title="Pullman Company">Pullman Company</a> became insolvent in 1969,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:147</sup> followed, in 1970, by the dominant railroad in the Northeastern United States, the <a href="/wiki/Penn_Central_Transportation_Company" title="Penn Central Transportation Company">Penn Central</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stover_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stover-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:234</sup> It now seemed that passenger rail's financial problems might bring down the railroad industry as a whole, yet few in government wanted to be held responsible for the extinction of the passenger train.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 1970, Congress passed, and President Richard Nixon signed into law, the Rail Passenger Service Act. Proponents of the bill, led by the <a href="/wiki/National_Association_of_Railroad_Passengers" title="National Association of Railroad Passengers">National Association of Railroad Passengers</a> (NARP), sought government funding to ensure the continuation of passenger trains. They conceived the National Railroad Passenger Corporation (NRPC), a hybrid public-private entity that would receive taxpayer funding and assume operation of intercity passenger trains. The original working <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brand_name" title="Brand name">brand name</a> for NRPC was Railpax, but shortly before the company started operating it was changed to Amtrak. There were several key provisions:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Any railroad operating intercity passenger service could contract with the NRPC, thereby joining the national system.</li>
<li>Participating railroads bought into the NRPC using a formula based on their recent intercity passenger losses. The purchase price could be satisfied either by cash or rolling stock; in exchange, the railroads received NRPC common stock.</li>
<li>Any participating railroad was freed of the obligation to operate intercity passenger service after May 1, 1971, except for those services chosen by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Transportation" title="United States Department of Transportation">Department of Transportation</a> (DOT) as part of a "basic system" of service and paid for by NRPC using its federal funds.</li>
<li>Railroads that chose not to join the NRPC system were required to continue operating their existing passenger service until 1975 and thenceforth had to pursue the customary ICC approval process for any discontinuance or alteration to the service.</li>
</ul>
<p>Of the 26 railroads still offering intercity passenger service in 1970, only six declined to join Amtrak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Nearly everyone involved expected the experiment to be short-lived. The Nixon administration and many Washington insiders viewed the NRPC as a politically expedient way for the President and Congress to give passenger trains a "last hurrah" as demanded by the public. They expected Amtrak to quietly disappear as public interest waned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> After <i><a href="/wiki/Fortune_(magazine)" title="Fortune (magazine)">Fortune</a></i> magazine exposed the manufactured mismanagement in 1974, Louis W. Menk, chairman of the <a href="/wiki/Burlington_Northern_Railroad" title="Burlington Northern Railroad">Burlington Northern Railroad</a>, remarked that the story was undermining the scheme to dismantle Amtrak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agreedsecretly_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-agreedsecretly-29">[29]</a></sup> Proponents also hoped that government intervention would be brief, but their view was that Amtrak would soon support itself. Neither view has proved correct. Popular support has allowed Amtrak to continue in operation longer than critics imagined, while financial results have made a return to private operation infeasible.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1970s:_the_Rainbow_Era">1970s: the Rainbow Era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 1970s: the Rainbow Era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Amtrak began operations on May 1, 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stover_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stover-26">[26]</a></sup> Amtrak received no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rail_tracks" title="Rail tracks">rail tracks</a> or <a href="/wiki/Right-of-way_(transportation)" title="Right-of-way (transportation)">right-of-way</a> at its inception. All Amtrak's routes were continuations of prior service, although Amtrak pruned about half the passenger rail network. Of the 364 trains operated previously, Amtrak only continued 182. On trains that continued, to the extent possible, schedules were retained with only minor changes from the <i><a href="/wiki/Official_Guide_of_the_Railways" title="Official Guide of the Railways">Official Guide of the Railways</a></i>, and under the same names.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Several major corridors became freight-only, including the ex-<a href="/wiki/New_York_Central_Railroad" title="New York Central Railroad">New York Central Railroad</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_Level_Route" title="Water Level Route">Water Level Route</a> across New York and Ohio and <a href="/wiki/Grand_Trunk_Western_Railroad" title="Grand Trunk Western Railroad">Grand Trunk Western Railroad</a>'s Chicago to Detroit route. Reduced passenger train schedules created headaches. A 19-hour layover became necessary for eastbound travel on the <i><a href="/wiki/James_Whitcomb_Riley_(train)" title="James Whitcomb Riley (train)">James Whitcomb Riley</a></i> between Chicago and <a href="/wiki/Newport_News,_Virginia" title="Newport News, Virginia">Newport News</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Amtrak inherited problems with train stations, most notably <a href="/wiki/Deferred_maintenance" title="Deferred maintenance">deferred maintenance</a>, and redundant facilities resulting from competing companies that served the same areas. On the day it started, Amtrak was given the responsibility of rerouting passenger trains from the seven train terminals in Chicago (<a href="/wiki/LaSalle_Street_Station" title="LaSalle Street Station">LaSalle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dearborn_Station" title="Dearborn Station">Dearborn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Central_Station_(Chicago)" title="Grand Central Station (Chicago)">Grand Central</a>, <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Station" title="Millennium Station">Randolph</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ogilvie_Transportation_Center" title="Ogilvie Transportation Center">Chicago Northwestern Terminal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Station_(Chicago_terminal)" title="Central Station (Chicago terminal)">Central</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Union_Station" title="Chicago Union Station">Union</a>) into just one, Union Station. In <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, Amtrak had to pay to maintain both <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Station_(New_York_City)" title="Pennsylvania Station (New York City)">Penn Station</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grand_Central_Terminal" title="Grand Central Terminal">Grand Central Terminal</a> because of the lack of track connections to bring trains from upstate New York into Penn Station, a problem not rectified until the building of the <a href="/wiki/West_Side_Line_(NYCRR)" title="West Side Line (NYCRR)">Empire Connection</a> in 1991.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Amtrak would abandon numerous large stations whose upkeep could no longer be justified. On the other hand, the creation of the Los Angeles–Seattle <i><a href="/wiki/Coast_Starlight" title="Coast Starlight">Coast Starlight</a></i> from three formerly separate trains was an immediate success.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Amtrak's early years are often called the <i>Rainbow Era</i>, which refers to the ad hoc arrangement of the rolling stock and locomotives from a pool of equipment, acquired by Amtrak, at its formation, that consisted of a large mix of paint schemes from their former owners. This rolling stock, which for the most part still bore the pre-Amtrak colors and logos, formed the multi-colored consists of early Amtrak trains. By mid-1971, Amtrak began purchasing some of the equipment it had leased, including 286 second-hand locomotives (of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electro-Motive_Division" title="Electro-Motive Division">EMD</a> E and F types, 30 <a href="/wiki/PRR_GG1" title="PRR GG1">GG1</a> electric locomotives) and 1,290 passenger cars, and continued leasing even more motive power. By 1975, the official Amtrak color scheme was painted on most Amtrak equipment and newly purchased locomotives and rolling stock began appearing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Amtrak soon had the opportunity to acquire <a href="/wiki/Right-of-way_(transportation)" title="Right-of-way (transportation)">rights-of-way</a>. Following the bankruptcy of several northeastern railroads in the early 1970s, including Penn Central, which owned and operated the <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Corridor" title="Northeast Corridor">Northeast Corridor</a> (NEC), Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Railroad_Revitalization_and_Regulatory_Reform_Act" title="Railroad Revitalization and Regulatory Reform Act">Railroad Revitalization and Regulatory Reform Act</a> of 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> A large part of the legislation was directed to the creation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consolidated_Rail_Corporation" title="Consolidated Rail Corporation">Conrail</a>, but the law also enabled the transfer of the portions of the NEC not already owned by state authorities to Amtrak. Amtrak acquired the majority of the NEC on April 1, 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> (The portion in Massachusetts is owned by the Commonwealth and managed by Amtrak. The route from New Haven to New Rochelle is owned by the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Transportation_Authority" title="Metropolitan Transportation Authority">Metropolitan Transportation Authority</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Department_of_Transportation" title="Connecticut Department of Transportation">Connecticut Department of Transportation</a> as the <a href="/wiki/New_Haven_Line" title="New Haven Line">New Haven Line</a>.)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This main line became Amtrak's "jewel" asset, and helped the railroad generate revenue. While the NEC ridership and revenues were higher than any other segment of the system, the cost of operating and maintaining the corridor proved to be overwhelming. As a result, Amtrak's federal subsidy was increased dramatically. In subsequent years, other short route segments not needed for freight operations were transferred to Amtrak.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In its first decade, Amtrak fell far short of financial independence, which continues today, but it did find modest success rebuilding trade. Outside factors discouraged competing transport, such as fuel shortages which increased costs of automobile and airline travel, and strikes which disrupted airline operations. Investments in Amtrak's track, equipment and information also made Amtrak more relevant to America's transportation needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-washpost-jones1_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-washpost-jones1-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csm-yemma1_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-csm-yemma1-34">[34]</a></sup> Amtrak's ridership increased from 16.6 million in 1972 to 21 million in 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NiceD_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-NiceD-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1980s_and_1990s">1980s and 1990s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 1980s and 1990s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acela_old_saybrook_ct_summer2011.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Acela_old_saybrook_ct_summer2011.jpg/220px-Acela_old_saybrook_ct_summer2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Acela_old_saybrook_ct_summer2011.jpg/330px-Acela_old_saybrook_ct_summer2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Acela_old_saybrook_ct_summer2011.jpg/440px-Acela_old_saybrook_ct_summer2011.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_California_Zephyr_Engines_1_and_56_Eastbound_at_Grand_Junction_-_img1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1936" data-file-width="2592" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Amtrak_California_Zephyr_Engines_1_and_56_Eastbound_at_Grand_Junction_-_img1.jpg/220px-Amtrak_California_Zephyr_Engines_1_and_56_Eastbound_at_Grand_Junction_-_img1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Amtrak_California_Zephyr_Engines_1_and_56_Eastbound_at_Grand_Junction_-_img1.jpg/330px-Amtrak_California_Zephyr_Engines_1_and_56_Eastbound_at_Grand_Junction_-_img1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Amtrak_California_Zephyr_Engines_1_and_56_Eastbound_at_Grand_Junction_-_img1.jpg/440px-Amtrak_California_Zephyr_Engines_1_and_56_Eastbound_at_Grand_Junction_-_img1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

In the 21st century Amtrak replaced its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F40PH" title="F40PH">F40PH</a> units with the <a href="/wiki/GE_Genesis" title="GE Genesis">GE Genesis</a>. Pictured are Amtrak engines #1 and #56, both <a href="/wiki/GE_Genesis" title="GE Genesis">GE Genesis</a> P42DC diesels, pulling the eastbound <i><a href="/wiki/California_Zephyr" title="California Zephyr">California Zephyr</a></i> at <a href="/wiki/Grand_Junction,_Colorado" title="Grand Junction, Colorado">Grand Junction, Colorado</a>, April 2012</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_-_GE_P42DC_-_Heritage_Phase_III_Livery.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1752" data-file-width="2336" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Amtrak_-_GE_P42DC_-_Heritage_Phase_III_Livery.JPG/220px-Amtrak_-_GE_P42DC_-_Heritage_Phase_III_Livery.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Amtrak_-_GE_P42DC_-_Heritage_Phase_III_Livery.JPG/330px-Amtrak_-_GE_P42DC_-_Heritage_Phase_III_Livery.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Amtrak_-_GE_P42DC_-_Heritage_Phase_III_Livery.JPG/440px-Amtrak_-_GE_P42DC_-_Heritage_Phase_III_Livery.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_-_GE_P42DC_-_Heritage_Phase_III_Livery.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Locomotive No. 66 at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Union_Station" title="Los Angeles Union Station">Los Angeles Union Station</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1982 former <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="United States Secretary of the Navy">Secretary of the Navy</a> and retired <a href="/wiki/Southern_Railway_(U.S.)" title="Southern Railway (U.S.)">Southern Railway</a> head <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W._Graham_Claytor,_Jr." title="W. Graham Claytor, Jr.">William Graham Claytor Jr.</a> became president. Claytor came out of retirement to lead Amtrak after the disastrous financial results during the Carter administration (1977–81).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Despite frequent clashes with the Reagan administration over funding, Claytor enjoyed a good relationship with <a href="/wiki/John_H._Riley" title="John H. Riley">John H. Riley</a>, the head of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Railroad_Administration" title="Federal Railroad Administration">Federal Railroad Administration</a> (FRA), and with members of Congress. Limited funding led Claytor to use <a href="/wiki/Money_market" title="Money market">short-term debt</a> to fund operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-web-claytor_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-web-claytor-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ridership stagnated at roughly 20 million passengers per year amid uncertain government aid from 1981 to about 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NiceD_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-NiceD-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amtrak-annual1999_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-amtrak-annual1999-38">[38]</a></sup> Thomas Downs succeeded Claytor in 1993. Amtrak's stated goal remained "operational self-sufficiency." By this time, however, Amtrak had a large overhang of debt from years of underfunding, and in the mid-1990s, Amtrak suffered through a serious cash crunch. Under Downs, Congress included a provision in the <a href="/wiki/Taxpayer_Relief_Act_of_1997" title="Taxpayer Relief Act of 1997">Taxpayer Relief Act of 1997</a> that resulted in Amtrak receiving a $2.3 billion tax refund that resolved their cash crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wapostmarch98_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-wapostmarch98-39">[39]</a></sup> However, Congress also instituted a "glide-path" to financial self-sufficiency, excluding railroad retirement tax act payments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scheinberg-test1_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-scheinberg-test1-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/George_Warrington" title="George Warrington">George Warrington</a> became president in 1998 with a mandate to make Amtrak financially self-sufficient. Passengers became "guests" and there were expansions into express freight work, but the financial plans failed. Amtrak's inroads in express freight delivery created additional friction with competing freight operators, including the <a href="/wiki/Trucking_industry_in_the_United_States" title="Trucking industry in the United States">trucking industry</a>. Delivery was delayed of much anticipated high-speed trainsets for the improved <i><a href="/wiki/Acela_Express" title="Acela Express">Acela Express</a></i> service, which promised to be a strong source of income and favorable publicity along the Northeast Corridor between Boston and Washington, D.C.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ridership increased during the first decade of the 21st century after implementation of capital improvements in the NEC and rises in automobile fuel costs. The inauguration of the <a href="/wiki/High-speed_rail" title="High-speed rail">high-speed</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Acela_Express" title="Acela Express">Acela Express</a></i> in late 2000 generated considerable publicity and led to major ridership gains. However, through the late 1990s and very early 21st century, Amtrak could not add sufficient express freight revenue or cut sufficient other expenditures to break even. By 2002, it was clear that Amtrak could not achieve self-sufficiency, but Congress continued to authorize funding and released Amtrak from the requirement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bg-wirzbicki1_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-bg-wirzbicki1-41">[41]</a></sup> In early 2002 <a href="/wiki/David_L._Gunn" title="David L. Gunn">David L. Gunn</a> replaced Warrington as president. In a departure from his predecessors' promises to make Amtrak self-sufficient in the short term, Gunn argued that no form of passenger transportation in the United States is self-sufficient as the economy is currently structured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dunn-test1_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-dunn-test1-42">[42]</a></sup> Highways, airports, and air traffic control <i>all</i> require large government expenditures to build and operate, coming from the <a href="/wiki/Highway_Trust_Fund" title="Highway Trust Fund">Highway Trust Fund</a> and <a href="/wiki/Airport_and_Airway_Trust_Fund" title="Airport and Airway Trust Fund">Aviation Trust Fund</a> paid for by user fees, highway fuel and road taxes, and, in the case of the General Fund, from general taxation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rnw-szep1_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-rnw-szep1-43">[43]</a></sup> Gunn dropped most freight express business and worked to eliminate <a href="/wiki/Deferred_maintenance" title="Deferred maintenance">deferred maintenance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ceeaan-gunn1_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-ceeaan-gunn1-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>A plan by the Bush administration "to privatize parts of the national passenger rail system and spin off other parts to partial state ownership" provoked disagreement within Amtrak's board of directors. Late in 2005 Gunn was fired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anytfiring_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-anytfiring-45">[45]</a></sup> Gunn's replacement, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Kummant" title="Alexander Kummant">Alexander Kummant</a> (2006–08), was committed to operating a national rail network, and, like Gunn, opposed the notion of putting the <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Corridor" title="Northeast Corridor">Northeast Corridor</a> under separate ownership,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt-wald2_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-wald2-46">[46]</a></sup> He said that shedding the system's long-distance routes would amount to selling national assets that are on par with national parks, and that Amtrak's abandonment of these routes would be irreversible. In late 2006, Amtrak unsuccessfully sought annual congressional funding of $1 billion for ten years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt-wald2_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-wald2-46">[46]</a></sup> In early 2007, Amtrak employed 20,000 people in 46 states and served 25 million passengers a year, its highest amount since its founding in 1970. <i><a href="/wiki/Politico" title="Politico">Politico</a></i> noted a key problem: "the rail system chronically operates in the red. A pattern has emerged: Congress overrides cutbacks demanded by the White House and appropriates enough funds to keep Amtrak from plunging into insolvency. But, Amtrak advocates say, that is not enough to fix the system's woes." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amtrackpolitico_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-amtrackpolitico-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Joseph_H._Boardman" title="Joseph H. Boardman">Joseph H. Boardman</a> replaced Kummant as President and CEO in late 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amtrak-BoardmanPres_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amtrak-BoardmanPres-48">[48]</a></sup> In 2011, Amtrak announced its intention to build a small segment of a <a href="/wiki/High-speed_rail" title="High-speed rail">high-speed rail</a> corridor from Penn Station in NYC, under the Hudson River in new tunnels, and double-tracking the line to Newark, NJ called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gateway_Project" title="Gateway Project">Gateway Project</a>, estimated to cost $13.5 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GatewayPDF_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-GatewayPDF-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> After years of almost revolving-door CEOs at Amtrak, in December 2013, Boardman was named "Railroader of the Year" by <i>Railway Age</i> magazine, which noted that with over five years in the job, he is the second-longest serving head of Amtrak since it was formed more than 40 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-railwayage_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-railwayage-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>From May 2011 to May 2012, Amtrak celebrated its 40th anniversary with festivities across the country that started on National Train Day (May 7, 2011). A commemorative book entitled <i>Amtrak: An American Story</i> was published, and a documentary was created. Four commemorative locomotives and a 40th Anniversary Exhibit Train toured the country. The Exhibit Train visited 45 communities and welcomed more than 85,000 visitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> It was an entirely rebuilt train powered by <a href="/wiki/GE_Genesis" title="GE Genesis">GE Genesis</a> locomotives and included three refurbished ex-Santa Fe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baggage_car" title="Baggage car">baggage cars</a> and a food service car. Four Genesis locomotives were painted into retired Amtrak paint schemes: No. 156 was in Phase 1 colors, No. 66 was in Phase 2 colors, No. 145 and No. 822 were in Phase 3 colors (822 pulled the Exhibit train),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> and No. 184 was in Phase 4 colors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crazytiger94_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-crazytiger94-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> In 2014 Amtrak began offering a "<a href="/wiki/Artist-in-residence" title="Artist-in-residence">residency</a>" program for writers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>On December 9, 2015, Boardman announced in a letter to employees that he would be leaving Amtrak in September 2016. He had advised the Amtrak Board of Directors of his decision the previous week. On August 19, 2016, the Amtrak Board of Directors named former <a href="/wiki/Norfolk_Southern_Railway" title="Norfolk Southern Railway">Norfolk Southern Railway</a> President &amp; CEO <a href="/wiki/Charles_Moorman" title="Charles Moorman">Charles "Wick" Moorman</a> as Boardman's successor with an effective date of September 1, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operations">Operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Routes">Routes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Routes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Amtrak_routes" title="List of Amtrak routes">List of Amtrak routes</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_busiest_Amtrak_stations" title="List of busiest Amtrak stations">List of busiest Amtrak stations</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_major_cities_in_U.S._lacking_Amtrak_service" title="List of major cities in U.S. lacking Amtrak service">List of major cities in U.S. lacking Amtrak service</a></div>
<p>Amtrak is no longer required by law to operate a national route system, but it is encouraged to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Amtrak has presence in 46 of the 48 contiguous states (lacking <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">South Dakota</a>). Amtrak services fall into three groups: short-haul service on the <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Corridor" title="Northeast Corridor">Northeast Corridor</a>, short-haul corridor service outside the Northeast Corridor, and long-distance service.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Service on the Northeast Corridor, between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boston,_Massachusetts" title="Boston, Massachusetts">Boston</a>, and Washington, D.C., as well as between <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harrisburg,_Pennsylvania" title="Harrisburg, Pennsylvania">Harrisburg</a>, is powered by overhead electric wires; for the rest of the system, diesel locomotives are used. Routes vary widely in frequency of service, from three-days-a-week trains on the <i><a href="/wiki/Sunset_Limited" title="Sunset Limited">Sunset Limited</a></i> to weekday service several times per hour on the <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Corridor" title="Northeast Corridor">Northeast Corridor</a> (NEC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amtrakschedules_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-amtrakschedules-60">[60]</a></sup> Amtrak also operates a captive bus service, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thruway_Motorcoach" title="Thruway Motorcoach">Thruway Motorcoach</a>, which provides connections to train routes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The most popular and heavily used services are those running on the NEC, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Acela_Express" title="Acela Express">Acela Express</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Northeast_Regional" title="Northeast Regional">Northeast Regional</a></i>. The NEC runs from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boston,_Massachusetts" title="Boston, Massachusetts">Boston</a> to <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> via <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>. Some services continue into <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>. The NEC services accounted for 11.4 million of Amtrak's 31.6 million passengers in fiscal year 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Railroad_Passenger_Corporation_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-National_Railroad_Passenger_Corporation-61">[61]</a></sup> Outside the NEC the most popular services are the short-haul corridors in California. These include the <i><a href="/wiki/Pacific_Surfliner" title="Pacific Surfliner">Pacific Surfliner</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Capitol_Corridor" title="Capitol Corridor">Capitol Corridor</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/San_Joaquin_(train)" title="San Joaquin (train)">San Joaquin</a></i>, supplemented by an extensive network of connecting buses. Together the California corridor trains accounted for a combined 5,627,000 passengers in fiscal year 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Railroad_Passenger_Corporation_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-National_Railroad_Passenger_Corporation-61">[61]</a></sup> Other popular corridors include the <i><a href="/wiki/Empire_Service_(train)" title="Empire Service (train)">Empire Service</a></i>, which operates between New York City and <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>, Ontario via <a href="/wiki/Albany,_New_York" title="Albany, New York">Albany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buffalo,_New_York" title="Buffalo, New York">Buffalo</a>, New York and carried about 1,538,000 passengers in 2015, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Keystone_Service" title="Keystone Service">Keystone Service</a></i> from New York City to <a href="/wiki/Harrisburg,_Pennsylvania" title="Harrisburg, Pennsylvania">Harrisburg</a>, Pennsylvania via <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> that carried 1,343,000 passengers in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Four of the six stations busiest by boardings are on the NEC: <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Station_(New_York_City)" title="Pennsylvania Station (New York City)">New York (Penn Station)</a> (first), <a href="/wiki/Union_Station_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Union Station (Washington, D.C.)">Washington (Union Station)</a> (second), <a href="/wiki/30th_Street_Station" title="30th Street Station">Philadelphia (30th Street Station)</a> (third), and <a href="/wiki/South_Station" title="South Station">Boston (South Station)</a> (sixth). The other two are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Union_Station_(Chicago)" title="Union Station (Chicago)">Chicago (Union Station)</a> (fourth) and <a href="/wiki/Union_Station_(Los_Angeles)" title="Union Station (Los Angeles)">Los Angeles (Union Station)</a> (fifth).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Efficiency">Efficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Efficiency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Per passenger mile, Amtrak is 30–40 percent more energy-efficient than commercial airlines and automobiles overall,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bts-energy_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-bts-energy-64">[64]</a></sup> though the exact figures for particular routes depend on load factor along with other variables. The electrified trains in the NEC are considerably more efficient than Amtrak's diesels and can feed energy captured from <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_brake#The_motor_as_a_brake" title="Regenerative brake">regenerative braking</a> back to the electrical grid. Passenger rail is also very competitive with other modes in terms of safety per mile.</p>
