<p>On-time performance is calculated differently for airlines than for Amtrak. A plane is considered on-time if it arrives within 15 minutes of the schedule. Amtrak uses a sliding scale, with trips under 250 miles (400 km) considered late if they are more than 10 minutes behind schedule, up to 30 minutes for trips over 551 miles (887 km) in length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bts-ontime_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-bts-ontime-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2005, Amtrak's carbon dioxide equivalent emissions per passenger kilometre were 0.116 kg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> For comparison, this is similar to a car with two people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> about twice as high as the UK rail average (where much more of the system is electrified),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> about four times the average US motorcoach,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> and about eight times a Finnish electric intercity train or fully loaded fifty-seat coach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> It is, however, about two thirds of the raw CO<sub>2</sub>-equivalent emissions of a long-distance domestic flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intermodal_connections">Intermodal connections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Intermodal connections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Intermodal_passenger_transport" title="Intermodal passenger transport">Intermodal</a> connections between Amtrak trains and other transportation are available at many stations. Most Amtrak rail stations in <a href="/wiki/Central_business_district" title="Central business district">downtown areas</a> have connections to local public transport. Amtrak also <a href="/wiki/Codeshare_agreement" title="Codeshare agreement">code shares</a> with <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a>, providing service between <a href="/wiki/Newark_Liberty_International_Airport" title="Newark Liberty International Airport">Newark Liberty International Airport</a> (via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newark_Liberty_International_Airport_(NJT_station)" title="Newark Liberty International Airport (NJT station)">its Amtrak station</a> and <a href="/wiki/AirTrain_Newark" title="AirTrain Newark">AirTrain Newark</a>) and <a href="/wiki/30th_Street_Station" title="30th Street Station">Philadelphia 30th St</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wilmington,_Delaware" title="Wilmington, Delaware">Wilmington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stamford,_Connecticut" title="Stamford, Connecticut">Stamford</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Union_Station_(New_Haven,_Connecticut)" title="Union Station (New Haven, Connecticut)">New Haven</a>. Special codes are used to designate these intermodal routes, such as "ZVE" to designate the combination of New Haven's Union Station and Newark International Airport and the Amtrak connection between them. Amtrak also serves airport stations at <a href="/wiki/General_Mitchell_International_Airport" title="General Mitchell International Airport">Milwaukee</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oakland_Coliseum_Amtrak/BART_Station" title="Oakland Coliseum Amtrak/BART Station">Oakland</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bob_Hope_Airport_Train_Station" title="Bob Hope Airport Train Station">Burbank</a>, and <a href="/wiki/BWI_Rail_Station" title="BWI Rail Station">Baltimore</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Amtrak coordinates <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thruway_Motorcoach" title="Thruway Motorcoach">Thruway Motorcoach</a> service to extend many of its routes, especially in California.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On-time_performance">On-time performance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: On-time performance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Outside the <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Corridor" title="Northeast Corridor">Northeast Corridor</a> and stretches of track in Southern California and Michigan, most Amtrak trains run on tracks owned and operated by privately owned freight railroads. Freight rail operators are required under federal law to give dispatching preference to Amtrak trains. Some freight railroads have been accused of violating or skirting these regulations, allegedly resulting in passenger trains waiting in sidings for an hour or longer while waiting for freight traffic to clear the track. The railroads' dispatching practices were investigated in 2008,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> resulting in stricter laws about train priority. Subsequently, Amtrak's overall on-time performance went up from 74.7% in fiscal 2008 to 84.7% in 2009, with long-distance trains and others outside the NEC seeing the greatest benefit. The <i><a href="/wiki/Missouri_River_Runner" title="Missouri River Runner">Missouri River Runner</a></i> jumped from 11% to 95%, becoming one of Amtrak's best performers. The <i><a href="/wiki/Texas_Eagle" title="Texas Eagle">Texas Eagle</a></i> went from 22.4% to 96.7%, and the <i><a href="/wiki/California_Zephyr" title="California Zephyr">California Zephyr</a></i>, with a 5% on-time record in 2008, went up to 78.3%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> This improved performance coincided with a general economic downturn, resulting in the lowest freight-rail traffic volumes since at least 1988, meaning less freight traffic to impede passenger traffic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ridership">Ridership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Ridership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Annual_Amtrak_Ridership_Graph_thru_FY2012.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="504" data-file-width="692" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Annual_Amtrak_Ridership_Graph_thru_FY2012.svg/220px-Annual_Amtrak_Ridership_Graph_thru_FY2012.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Annual_Amtrak_Ridership_Graph_thru_FY2012.svg/330px-Annual_Amtrak_Ridership_Graph_thru_FY2012.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Annual_Amtrak_Ridership_Graph_thru_FY2012.svg/440px-Annual_Amtrak_Ridership_Graph_thru_FY2012.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Annual_Amtrak_Ridership_Graph_thru_FY2012.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Annual Ridership by fiscal year 1971–2012</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Amtrak carried 15,848,327 passengers in 1972, its first full year of operation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-narp-ridership_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-narp-ridership-78">[78]</a></sup> Ridership peaked at 31,240,565 in 2012, double the total in 1972.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amtrak-ridership-2012_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-amtrak-ridership-2012-79">[79]</a></sup> Total ridership in 2014 was 30,921,274.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amtrak-ridership-2014_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-amtrak-ridership-2014-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Guest_Rewards">Guest Rewards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Guest Rewards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Amtrak's <a href="/wiki/Loyalty_program" title="Loyalty program">loyalty program</a>, Guest Rewards,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amtrakrewards_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-amtrakrewards-81">[81]</a></sup> is similar to the <a href="/wiki/Frequent-flyer_program" title="Frequent-flyer program">frequent-flyer programs</a> of many airlines. Guest Rewards members accumulate points by riding Amtrak and through other activities, and can redeem these points for free or discounted Amtrak tickets and other rewards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amtrakrewards_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-amtrakrewards-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commuter_services">Commuter services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Commuter services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Commuter_rail_in_North_America" title="Commuter rail in North America">Commuter rail in North America</a></div>
<p>Through various commuter services, Amtrak serves an additional 61.1 million passengers per year in conjunction with state and regional authorities in California (through <a href="/wiki/Amtrak_California" title="Amtrak California">Amtrak California</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metrolink_(Southern_California)" title="Metrolink (Southern California)">Metrolink</a>), Connecticut (through <a href="/wiki/Shore_Line_East" title="Shore Line East">Shore Line East</a>), and Maryland (through <a href="/wiki/MARC_Train" title="MARC Train">MARC</a>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lines">Lines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Lines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="mw-stack" style="box-sizing: border-box; float:right;">
<div style="overflow: hidden; margin: 1px;">
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_Catenary_Maintenance_Vehicle_(4982606318).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Amtrak_Catenary_Maintenance_Vehicle_%284982606318%29.jpg/220px-Amtrak_Catenary_Maintenance_Vehicle_%284982606318%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Amtrak_Catenary_Maintenance_Vehicle_%284982606318%29.jpg/330px-Amtrak_Catenary_Maintenance_Vehicle_%284982606318%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Amtrak_Catenary_Maintenance_Vehicle_%284982606318%29.jpg/440px-Amtrak_Catenary_Maintenance_Vehicle_%284982606318%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_Catenary_Maintenance_Vehicle_(4982606318).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An Amtrak catenary maintenance vehicle on the <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Corridor" title="Northeast Corridor">Northeast Corridor</a> in <a href="/wiki/Guilford,_Connecticut" title="Guilford, Connecticut">Guilford, Connecticut</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_Regional_viewed_from_NJ_Transit_train.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Amtrak_Regional_viewed_from_NJ_Transit_train.jpg/220px-Amtrak_Regional_viewed_from_NJ_Transit_train.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Amtrak_Regional_viewed_from_NJ_Transit_train.jpg/330px-Amtrak_Regional_viewed_from_NJ_Transit_train.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Amtrak_Regional_viewed_from_NJ_Transit_train.jpg/440px-Amtrak_Regional_viewed_from_NJ_Transit_train.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_Regional_viewed_from_NJ_Transit_train.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An electric Amtrak train with two <a href="/wiki/EMD_AEM-7" title="EMD AEM-7">AEM-7</a> locomotives running through New Jersey on the Northeast Corridor</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Along the NEC and in several other areas, Amtrak owns 730 miles (1,170 km) including 17 tunnels consisting of 29.7 miles (47.8 km) of track, and 1,186 bridges (including the famous <a href="/wiki/Hell_Gate_Bridge" title="Hell Gate Bridge">Hell Gate Bridge</a>) consisting of 42.5 miles (68.4 km) of track. In several places, primarily in New England, Amtrak leases tracks, providing track maintenance and controlling train movements. Most often, these tracks are leased from state, regional, or local governments. Amtrak owns and operates the following lines:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Northeast_Corridor" title="Northeast Corridor">Northeast Corridor</a>: the Northeast Corridor between Washington, D.C. and Boston via <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newark,_New_Jersey" title="Newark, New Jersey">Newark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transportation_in_New_York_City" title="Transportation in New York City">New York</a> and <a href="/wiki/Providence,_Rhode_Island" title="Providence, Rhode Island">Providence</a> is largely owned by Amtrak, working cooperatively with several state and regional commuter agencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vision2012_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-vision2012-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amtrakhighspeed_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-amtrakhighspeed-84">[84]</a></sup> Amtrak owns the majority of the corridor. Between New Haven, Connecticut, and New Rochelle, New York, Northeast Corridor trains travel on the <a href="/wiki/Metro-North_Railroad" title="Metro-North Railroad">Metro-North Railroad</a>'s <a href="/wiki/New_Haven_Line" title="New Haven Line">New Haven Line</a>, which is owned and operated by the <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Department_of_Transportation" title="Connecticut Department of Transportation">Connecticut Department of Transportation</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Transportation_Authority" title="Metropolitan Transportation Authority">Metropolitan Transportation Authority</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_to_Harrisburg_Main_Line" title="Philadelphia to Harrisburg Main Line">Philadelphia to Harrisburg Main Line</a>: the Philadelphia to Harrisburg Main Line runs from Philadelphia to <a href="/wiki/Harrisburg,_Pennsylvania" title="Harrisburg, Pennsylvania">Harrisburg, Pennsylvania</a>. As a result of an investment partnership with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Pennsylvania" title="Commonwealth of Pennsylvania">Commonwealth of Pennsylvania</a>, signal and track improvements were completed in October 2006 that allow all-electric service with a top speed of 110 miles per hour (180 km/h) to run along the corridor.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Empire_Corridor" title="Empire Corridor">Empire Corridor</a>: Amtrak owns the 11 miles (18 km) between <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Station_(New_York_City)" title="Pennsylvania Station (New York City)">New York Penn Station</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spuyten_Duyvil,_Bronx" title="Spuyten Duyvil, Bronx">Spuyten Duyvil, New York</a>. In 2012 leased the 94.5 miles (152 km) between <a href="/wiki/Poughkeepsie,_New_York" title="Poughkeepsie, New York">Poughkeepsie, New York</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Schenectady,_New_York" title="Schenectady, New York">Schenectady, New York</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> In addition, Amtrak owns the tracks across the <a href="/wiki/Whirlpool_Rapids_Bridge" title="Whirlpool Rapids Bridge">Whirlpool Rapids Bridge</a> and short approach sections near it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Haven-Springfield_Line" title="New Haven-Springfield Line">New Haven-Springfield Line</a>: Amtrak owns the 60.5 miles (97.4 km) line between <a href="/wiki/New_Haven,_Connecticut" title="New Haven, Connecticut">New Haven</a> and <a href="/wiki/Springfield,_Massachusetts" title="Springfield, Massachusetts">Springfield</a>.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicago%E2%80%93Detroit_Line" title="Chicago–Detroit Line">Chicago–Detroit Line</a>: Amtrak acquired the west end of the former <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Central_Railroad" title="Michigan Central Railroad">Michigan Central</a> main line from <a href="/wiki/Conrail" title="Conrail">Conrail</a> in 1976.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Post_Road_Branch" title="Post Road Branch">Post Road Branch</a>: 12.42 miles (19.99 km), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Post_Road_Junction&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Post Road Junction (page does not exist)">Post Road Junction</a> to <a href="/wiki/Rensselaer,_New_York" title="Rensselaer, New York">Rensselaer, New York</a></li>
</ul>
<p>In addition to these lines Amtrak owns station and yard tracks in Chicago, Los Angeles, New Orleans, New York City, <a href="/wiki/Oakland,_California" title="Oakland, California">Oakland</a> (Kirkham Street Yard), <a href="/wiki/Orlando,_Florida" title="Orlando, Florida">Orlando</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portland,_Oregon" title="Portland, Oregon">Portland, Oregon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Paul,_Minnesota" title="Saint Paul, Minnesota">Saint Paul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a>, and Washington, D.C. Amtrak leases station and yard tracks in <a href="/wiki/Hialeah,_Florida" title="Hialeah, Florida">Hialeah</a>, near Miami, Florida, from the State of Florida.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Amtrak owns the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Union_Station_Company" title="Chicago Union Station Company">Chicago Union Station Company</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Union_Station_(Chicago)" title="Union Station (Chicago)">Chicago Union Station</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Station_(New_York_City)" title="Pennsylvania Station (New York City)">New York Penn Station</a>. It has a 99.7% interest in the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Terminal_Company" title="Washington Terminal Company">Washington Terminal Company</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> (tracks around <a href="/wiki/Union_Station_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Union Station (Washington, D.C.)">Washington Union Station</a>) and 99% of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=30th_Street_Limited&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="30th Street Limited (page does not exist)">30th Street Limited</a> (Philadelphia <a href="/wiki/30th_Street_Station" title="30th Street Station">30th Street Station</a>). Also owned by Amtrak is Passenger Railroad Insurance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rolling_stock">Rolling stock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Rolling stock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Amtrak_rolling_stock" title="List of Amtrak rolling stock">List of Amtrak rolling stock</a></div>
<p>Amtrak owns 2,142 railway cars and 425 locomotives for revenue runs and service. Examples include the <a href="/wiki/GE_Genesis" title="GE Genesis">GE P42DC</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Siemens_ACS-64" title="Siemens ACS-64">Siemens ACS-64</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Amfleet" title="Amfleet">Amfleet</a> car and the <a href="/wiki/Superliner_(railcar)" title="Superliner (railcar)">Superliner</a> car. Occasionally private cars, or loaned locomotives from other railroads can be found on Amtrak trains.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="On-board_services">On-board services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: On-board services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classes_of_service">Classes of service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Classes of service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="mw-stack" style="box-sizing: border-box; float:right;">
<div style="overflow: hidden; margin: 1px;">
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Viewliner_(13970131747).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="4608" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Viewliner_%2813970131747%29.jpg/220px-Viewliner_%2813970131747%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Viewliner_%2813970131747%29.jpg/330px-Viewliner_%2813970131747%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Viewliner_%2813970131747%29.jpg/440px-Viewliner_%2813970131747%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Viewliner_(13970131747).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The interior of a <a href="/wiki/Viewliner" title="Viewliner">Viewliner</a> sleeping car bedroom with the lower bed down</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amfleet_(14156927994).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="4608" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Amfleet_%2814156927994%29.jpg/220px-Amfleet_%2814156927994%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Amfleet_%2814156927994%29.jpg/330px-Amfleet_%2814156927994%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Amfleet_%2814156927994%29.jpg/440px-Amfleet_%2814156927994%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amfleet_(14156927994).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The interior of a long-distance <a href="/wiki/Amfleet" title="Amfleet">Amfleet II</a> coach</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> Amtrak offers four classes of service: First Class, Sleeper Service, Business Class, and Coach Class:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amtrak_Seat_Plan_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amtrak_Seat_Plan-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b>First Class</b>: First Class service is currently offered only on the <i><a href="/wiki/Acela_Express" title="Acela Express">Acela Express</a></i>. Seats are larger than those of Business Class and come in a variety of seating styles (single, facing singles with table, double, facing doubles with table and wheelchair accessible). First Class is located in a separate car from business class and is located at the end of the train (to reduce the number of passengers walking in the aisles). A car attendant provides passengers with hot towel service, a complimentary meal and alcoholic beverages. First Class passengers have access to ClubAcela lounges located at select stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Sleeper Service</b>: Sleeper Service comprises private room accommodations on long-distance trains. Rooms are classified into roomettes, bedrooms, accessible bedrooms, and family bedrooms (on some trains). Included in the price of a room are full meals and attendant service. At night, attendants convert rooms into sleeping areas with fold-down beds and fresh linens. Shower facilities with towels and bar soap are available. Complimentary juice, coffee and bottled water are included as well. Sleeper car passengers have access to all passenger facilities aboard the train. Sleeper passengers have access to ClubAcela lounges, Metropolitan Lounges, and unattended First Class Lounges located at select stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Business Class</b>: Business Class seating is offered on the <i>Acela Express</i>, <i>Northeast Regional</i>, many short-haul corridor trains and some long-distance trains. Business Class is located in a dedicated car or section of the train. While the specific features vary by route, many include extra legroom and complimentary non-alcoholic drinks. Seats in business class recline, are typically appointed in leather and feature a fold-down tray table, foot rest, individual reading light, power outlet. Business Class passengers have access to Metropolitan Lounges located at select stations and may purchase a daily access pass to select ClubAcela locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Coach Class</b>: Coach Class is the standard class of service on all Amtrak trains except the <i>Acela Express</i>. Seats in coach recline and feature a fold-down tray table, foot rest, individual reading light, and power outlet. Coach cars on long-distance trains are configured with fewer seats per car so that passengers have additional legroom and seats are equipped with leg rests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="WiFi_and_electronic_services">WiFi and electronic services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: WiFi and electronic services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Amtrak launched an <a href="/wiki/Electronic_ticket" title="Electronic ticket">e-ticketing</a> system on the <i><a href="/wiki/Downeaster_(train)" title="Downeaster (train)">Downeaster</a></i> in November 2011,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etix_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-etix-94">[94]</a></sup> and rolled it out nationwide on July 30, 2012. Amtrak officials said the system gives "more accurate knowledge in realtime of who is on the train which greatly improves the safety and security of passengers; en route reporting of onboard equipment problems to mechanical crews which may result in faster resolution of the issue; and more efficient financial reporting."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natetickets_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-natetickets-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>Amtrak first offered free <a href="/wiki/Wi-Fi" title="Wi-Fi">Wi-Fi</a> service to passengers aboard the <i>Downeaster</i> in 2008, the <i><a href="/wiki/Acela_Express" title="Acela Express">Acela Express</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Northeast_Regional" title="Northeast Regional">Northeast Regional</a></i> trains on the NEC in 2010, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Amtrak_Cascades" title="Amtrak Cascades">Amtrak Cascades</a></i> in 2011. In February 2014, Amtrak rolled out Wi-Fi on corridor trains out of Chicago. When all the Midwest cars offer the AmtrakConnect service, about 85% of all Amtrak passengers nationwide will have Wi-Fi access.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amtrakwikif_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-amtrakwikif-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> As of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, most Amtrak passengers have access to free Wi-Fi. The service has developed a reputation for being unreliable and slow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gulliver_2011_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-gulliver_2011-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nixon_2012_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-nixon_2012-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baggage">Baggage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Baggage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_Baggage_Car_(6138458661).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Amtrak_Baggage_Car_%286138458661%29.jpg/220px-Amtrak_Baggage_Car_%286138458661%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Amtrak_Baggage_Car_%286138458661%29.jpg/330px-Amtrak_Baggage_Car_%286138458661%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Amtrak_Baggage_Car_%286138458661%29.jpg/440px-Amtrak_Baggage_Car_%286138458661%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_Baggage_Car_(6138458661).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A typical Amtrak baggage car in Chicago</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Amtrak allows carry-on baggage on all routes; services with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baggage_car" title="Baggage car">baggage cars</a> allow checked baggage at selected stations.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> With the passage of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wicker_Amendment" title="Wicker Amendment">Wicker Amendment</a> in 2010 passengers are allowed to put lawfully owned, unloaded firearms in checked Amtrak baggage, reversing a decade-long ban on such carriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Amtrak_Express" title="Amtrak Express">Amtrak Express</a> (<a href="/wiki/Reporting_mark" title="Reporting mark">reporting marks</a> <b>AMTK</b>, <b>AMTZ</b>) provides small-package and less-than-truckload shipping among more than 100 cities. Amtrak Express also offers station-to-station shipment of human remains to many express cities. At smaller stations, funeral directors must load and unload the shipment onto and off the train. Amtrak hauled mail for the United States Postal Service and time-sensitive freight, but canceled these services in October 2004 due to minuscule profits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> On most parts of the few lines that Amtrak owns, <a href="/wiki/Arrangements_between_railroads" title="Arrangements between railroads">trackage-rights</a> agreements allow freight railroads to use its trackage.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Labor_issues">Labor issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Labor issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the modern era, Amtrak faces a number of important labor issues. In the area of pension funding, because of limitations originally imposed by Congress, most Amtrak workers were traditionally classified as "railroad employees" and contributions to the Railroad Retirement system have been made for those employees. However, because the size of the contributions is determined on an industry-wide basis rather than with reference to the employer for whom the employees work, some critics, such as the <a href="/wiki/National_Association_of_Railroad_Passengers" title="National Association of Railroad Passengers">National Association of Railroad Passengers</a>, maintain that Amtrak is subsidizing freight railroad pensions by as much as US$150 million/year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>In recent times, efforts at reforming passenger rail have addressed labor issues. In 1997 Congress released Amtrak from a prohibition on contracting for labor outside the corporation (and outside its unions), opening the door to privatization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Since that time, many of Amtrak's employees have been working without a contract. The most recent contract, signed in 1999, was mainly retroactive.</p>
<p>Because of the fragmentation of railroad unions by job, as of 2009<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> Amtrak has 14 separate unions to negotiate with. Plus, it has 24 separate contracts with those unions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-redortibit_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-redortibit-125">[125]</a></sup> This makes it difficult to make substantial changes, in contrast to a situation where one union negotiates with one employer. Former Amtrak president Kummant followed a cooperative posture with Amtrak's trade unions, ruling out plans to privatize large parts of Amtrak's unionized workforce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wanty2006_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-wanty2006-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>In late 2007 and early 2008, however, major labor issues arose, a result of a dispute between Amtrak and 16 unions regarding which employees should receive healthcare benefits.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The dispute was not resolved quickly, and the situation escalated to the point of President Bush declaring a Presidential Emergency Board to resolve the issues. It was not immediately successful, and a strike was threatened to begin on January 30, 2008. In the middle of that month, however, it was announced that Amtrak and the unions had come to terms and January 30 passed without a strike. In late February it was announced that three more unions had worked out their differences, and as of that time it seemed unlikely that any more issues would arise in the near future.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_funding">Public funding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Public funding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Amtrak receives annual appropriations from federal and state governments to supplement operating and capital programs.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_funding">History of funding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: History of funding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1970s_to_1990s">1970s to 1990s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: 1970s to 1990s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Amtrak commenced operations in 1971 with $40 million in direct federal aid, $100 million in federally insured loans, and a somewhat larger private contribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Officials expected that Amtrak would break even by 1974, but those expectations proved unrealistic and annual direct federal aid reached a 17-year high in 1981 of $1.25 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Ronald_Reagan" title="Presidency of Ronald Reagan">Reagan administration</a>, appropriations were halved and by 1986, federal support fell to a decade low of $601 million, almost none of which were capital appropriations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1995manual_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-1995manual-131">[131]</a></sup> In the late 1980s and early 1990s, Congress continued the reductionist trend even while Amtrak expenses held steady or rose. Amtrak was forced to borrow to meet short-term operating needs, and by 1995 Amtrak was on the brink of a cash crisis and was unable to continue to service its debts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1999reportp41_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-1999reportp41-132">[132]</a></sup> In response, in 1997 Congress authorized $5.2 billion for Amtrak over the next five years – largely to complete the <i>Acela</i> capital project – on the condition that Amtrak submit to the ultimatum of self-sufficiency by 2003 or liquidation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> While Amtrak made financial improvements during this period,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> it did not achieve self-sufficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-railroadingscuts_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-railroadingscuts-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2000s">2000s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: 2000s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2004, a stalemate in federal support of Amtrak forced cutbacks in services and routes as well as resumption of deferred maintenance. In fiscal 2004 and 2005, Congress appropriated about $1.2 billion for Amtrak, $300 million more than President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> had requested. However, the company's board requested $1.8 billion through fiscal 2006, the majority of which (about $1.3 billion) would be used to bring infrastructure, rolling stock, and motive power back to a state of good repair. In Congressional testimony, the DOT Inspector General confirmed that Amtrak would need at least $1.4 billion to $1.5 billion in fiscal 2006 and $2 billion in fiscal 2007 just to maintain the status quo. In 2006, Amtrak received just under $1.4 billion, with the condition that Amtrak would reduce (but not eliminate) food and sleeper service losses. Thus, dining service was simplified and now requires two fewer on-board service workers. Only <i><a href="/wiki/Auto_Train" title="Auto Train">Auto Train</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Empire_Builder" title="Empire Builder">Empire Builder</a></i> services continue regular made-on-board meal service. In 2010 the Senate approved a bill to provide $1.96 billion to Amtrak, but cut the approval for high-speed rail to a $1 billion appropriation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-railroadingscuts_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-railroadingscuts-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_Cascades_cars.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Amtrak_Cascades_cars.jpg/220px-Amtrak_Cascades_cars.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Amtrak_Cascades_cars.jpg/330px-Amtrak_Cascades_cars.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Amtrak_Cascades_cars.jpg/440px-Amtrak_Cascades_cars.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amtrak_Cascades_cars.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Amtrak_Cascades" title="Amtrak Cascades">Amtrak Cascades</a></i> service with tilting <a href="/wiki/Talgo" title="Talgo">Talgo</a> trainsets in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Street_Station_(Seattle)" title="King Street Station (Seattle)">Seattle, Washington</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NCF59.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1104" data-file-width="1772" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/NCF59.jpg/220px-NCF59.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/NCF59.jpg/330px-NCF59.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/NCF59.jpg/440px-NCF59.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NCF59.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Amtrak's <i><a href="/wiki/Piedmont_(train)" title="Piedmont (train)">Piedmont</a></i> near <a href="/wiki/Charlotte,_North_Carolina" title="Charlotte, North Carolina">Charlotte, North Carolina</a> with a state-owned locomotive. This route is run under a partnership with the <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_Department_of_Transportation" title="North Carolina Department of Transportation">North Carolina Department of Transportation</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>State governments have partially filled the breach left by reductions in federal aid. Several states have entered into operating partnerships with Amtrak, notably California, Pennsylvania, Illinois, Michigan, Oregon, Missouri, <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>, North Carolina, Oklahoma, Texas, Wisconsin, Vermont, Maine, and New York, as well as the Canadian province of <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a>, which provides some of the resources for the operation of the <i><a href="/wiki/Amtrak_Cascades" title="Amtrak Cascades">Cascades</a></i> route.</p>
<p>With the dramatic rise in gasoline prices during 2007–08, Amtrak has seen record ridership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap-karush1_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-ap-karush1-135">[135]</a></sup> Capping a steady five-year increase in ridership overall, regional lines saw 12% year-over-year growth in May 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rnw-szep2_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-rnw-szep2-136">[136]</a></sup> In October 2007, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a> passed S-294, <i>Passenger Rail Improvement and Investment Act of 2007</i> (70–22) sponsored by Senators <a href="/wiki/Frank_Lautenberg" title="Frank Lautenberg">Frank Lautenberg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trent_Lott" title="Trent Lott">Trent Lott</a>. Despite a veto threat by President Bush, a similar bill passed the House on June 11, 2008, with a veto-proof margin (311–104).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap-karush2_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-ap-karush2-137">[137]</a></sup> The final bill, spurred on by the <a href="/wiki/2008_Chatsworth_train_collision" title="2008 Chatsworth train collision">September 12 Metrolink collision</a> in California and retitled <i><a href="/wiki/Rail_Safety_Improvement_Act_of_2008" title="Rail Safety Improvement Act of 2008">Rail Safety Improvement Act of 2008</a></i>, was signed into law by President Bush on October 16, 2008. The bill appropriates $2.6 billion a year in Amtrak funding through 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lat-hymon1_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-lat-hymon1-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2010s">2010s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: 2010s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Amtrak points out that in 2010, its farebox recovery (percentage of operating costs covered by revenues generated by passenger fares) was 79%, the highest reported for any U.S. passenger railroad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Railroad_Passenger_Corporation_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-National_Railroad_Passenger_Corporation-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Amtrak has argued that it needs to increase capital program costs in 2013 in order to replace old train equipment because the multi-year maintenance costs for those trains exceeds what it would cost to simply buy new equipment that would not need to be repaired for several years. However, despite an initial request for more than $2.1 billion in funding for the year, the company had to deal with a year-over-year cut in 2013 federal appropriations, dropping to under $1.4 billion for the first time in several years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-127">[127]</a></sup> Amtrak stated in 2010 that the backlog of needed repairs of the track it owns on the <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Corridor" title="Northeast Corridor">Northeast Corridor</a> included over 200 bridges, most dating to the 19th century, tunnels under Baltimore dating to the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> Era and functionally obsolete <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Track_switch" title="Track switch">track switches</a> which would cost $5.2 billion to repair (more than triple Amtrak's total annual budget).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-comprehenfy13_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-comprehenfy13-139">[139]</a></sup> Amtrak's budget is only allocated on a yearly basis, and it has been argued by Joseph Vranich that this makes multi-year development programs and long-term fiscal planning difficult if not impossible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vranich_2004_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vranich_2004-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In Fiscal Year 2011, the U.S. Congress granted Amtrak $563 million for operating and $922 million for capital programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy">Controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amtrak&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Government aid to Amtrak was controversial from the beginning. The formation of Amtrak in 1971 was criticized as a bailout serving corporate rail interests and union railroaders, not the traveling public. Critics have asserted that Amtrak has proven incapable of operating as a business and that it does not provide valuable transportation services meriting public support,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vranich_2004_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vranich_2004-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> a "mobile money-burning machine."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Many argued that subsidies should be ended, national rail service terminated, and the NEC turned over to private interests. "To fund a <i>Nostalgia Limited</i> is not in the public interest."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canamtraksurvive_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-canamtraksurvive-143">[143]</a></sup> Critics also question Amtrak's energy efficiency,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FedAmtrak_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FedAmtrak-144">[144]</a></sup> though the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">U.S. Department of Energy</a> considers Amtrak among the most energy-efficient forms of transportation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newsandmediaamtrak_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-newsandmediaamtrak-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>Proponents<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (June 2015)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> have pointed out that the government heavily subsidizes the <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Highway_System" title="Interstate Highway System">Interstate Highway System</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aviation_Administration" title="Federal Aviation Administration">Federal Aviation Administration</a>, many airports, among many aspects of passenger aviation. Massive government aid to those forms of travel was a primary factor in the decline of passenger service on privately owned railroads in the 1950s and 1960s. In addition, Amtrak pays property taxes (through fees to host railroads) that highway users do not pay. Advocates have asserted that Amtrak should only be expected to be as self-sufficient as those competing modes of transit.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Along these lines, in a June 2008 interview with Reuters,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rnw-szep1_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-rnw-szep1-43">[43]</a></sup> Amtrak President Alex Kummant made specific observations: $10 billion per year is transferred from the general fund to the <a href="/wiki/Highway_Trust_Fund" title="Highway Trust Fund">Highway Trust Fund</a>; $2.7 billion is granted to the FAA; $8 billion goes to "security and life safety for cruise ships."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rnw-szep1_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-rnw-szep1-43">[43]</a></sup> Kummant makes a comparison of public subsidies: "it's like $40 a passenger for Amtrak and $500 to $700 per automobile." Moreover, Amtrak provides all of its own security, while airport security is a separate federal subsidy. Kummant added: "Let's not even get into airport construction which is a miasma of state, federal and local tax breaks and tax refinancing and God knows what."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rnw-szep1_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-rnw-szep1-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Rail Passenger Service Act of 1970, which established Amtrak, specifically states that, "The Corporation will not be an agency or establishment of the United States Government".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-firstamtrak_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-firstamtrak-146">[146]</a></sup> Then <a href="/wiki/Common_stock" title="Common stock">common stock</a> was issued in 1971 to railroads that contributed capital and equipment; these shares convey almost no benefits,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thepastandfuture_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-thepastandfuture-147">[147]</a></sup> but their current holders<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> declined a 2002 buy-out offer by Amtrak. There are currently 109,396,994 shares of preferred stock, at a par value of $100 per share, all held by the US government. There are currently 9,385,694 shares of common stock, with a par value of $10 per share, held by four other railroad companies: APU (formerly Penn Central) 53%, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BNSF" title="BNSF">BNSF</a> (35%), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_Pacific" title="Canadian Pacific">Canadian Pacific</a> (7%), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_National" title="Canadian National">Canadian National</a> (5%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-objectcato_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-objectcato-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>Supporters of Amtrak and transit systems also point out that as the first decade of the 21st century came to a close, the user fees from the Highway Trust Fund did not cover the cost of maintaining the national highway system.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Transportation advocacy groups, such as the American Association of State Highway and Transportation Officials, have noted that since 2008, the U.S. Congress has authorized transfers of money from the General Fund to the Highway Trust Fund, to keep it solvent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aashto2011impossible_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-aashto2011impossible-150">[150]</a></sup> Transfers from the General Fund to the Highway Trust Fund include: $8 billion (FY 2008), $7 billion (FY 2009), and $19.5 billion (FY 2010).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aashto2011impossible_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-aashto2011impossible-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
